Definition of al-Tawhid and Its Central Importance
Al-Laqqani defines legal tawhid and explains its role in worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal definition of tawhid as singling out Allah in worship and belief, and why it is the foundation of all acts of worship.

Core Idea
True tawhid unites action and belief, making it the most honorable and essential act of worship.

In this passage, Al-Laqqani provides a concise but comprehensive definition of tawhid. He combines both the outward act of worship and the inner conviction, and then highlights why this concept holds the highest rank in Islam. Let us read his words closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Focus on the flow from definition to implications to importance.
2
Identify the two components of tawhid
Notice that the first sentence mentions both 'إفراد المعبود بالعبادة' (action) and 'اعتقاد وحدانية' (belief). This shows tawhid is not merely a verbal statement.
The commentary clarifies this dual nature.
3
Understand the implications and significance
The passage then explains what oneness means for Allah's essence, attributes, and actions. Finally, it states that tawhid is the most honorable worship, a condition for validity, and the cause of salvation.
Reflect on why tawhid is given such a central role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التوحيد الشرعي وهو إفراد المعبود بالعبادة مع اعتقاد وحدانية ذاته وصفاته وأفعاله فلا تقبل ذاته الانقسام بوجه ولا تشبه صفاته الصفات ولا تدخل أفعاله الاشتراك وقيل التوحيد إثبات ذات غير مشبهة بالذوات ولا معطلة عن الصفات وتخصيص الإرسال بالتوحيد لانه شرف العبادات وأفضل الطاعات وشرط في صحتها وسبب النجاة من العذاب المخلد
The legal tawhid is: singling out the worshipped one with worship while believing in the oneness of His essence, His attributes, and His actions. So His essence does not accept division in any way, nor do His attributes resemble attributes, nor do His actions enter into partnership. And it is said: tawhid is affirming an essence not resembling essences nor divested of attributes. And the specification of sending (prophets) with tawhid is because it is the honor of acts of worship, the best of obediences, a condition for their validity, and a cause of salvation from eternal punishment.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines al-tawhid al-shar'i (legal monotheism) as the exclusive devotion of worship to Allah combined with belief in the oneness of His essence, attributes, and actions. It begins by stating that tawhid requires both action (إفراد العبادة) and belief (اعتقاد). Then it clarifies the implications: Allah's essence cannot be divided, His attributes are incomparable to created attributes, and His actions have no partner or associate. A second definition is given: tawhid is affirming a unique essence that neither resembles other essences (denying anthropomorphism) nor is stripped of attributes (denying ta'til). Finally, the passage emphasizes the paramount importance of tawhid: it is the most honorable act of worship, the best of all obediences, a necessary condition for the validity of any act of worship, and the means of salvation from eternal punishment. Thus tawhid is not merely one doctrine among others but the foundation and criterion of all religious practice.

Key Terms
التوحيد الشرعي
إفراد المعبود
وحدانية
اعتقاد
ذات
صفات
أفعال
اشتراك
شرف العبادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawhid is only a verbal declaration or a mere theological concept without practical implications.
Correction: The passage shows tawhid includes both action (إفراد العبادة) and belief, making it a comprehensive state that governs all worship and is essential for its validity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what role does tawhid play in acts of worship?
A) It is recommended but not obligatory.
B) It is a condition for their validity.
C) It is only for prophets and saints.
D) It applies only to verbal declarations.

Key Takeaway
Tawhid is not just a belief but a lived reality that validates all worship and secures salvation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform any act of worship—prayer, charity, fasting—is your intention pure for Allah alone, or do you find traces of showing off or seeking human praise? Reflect on the passage's condition: tawhid is required for validity. What hidden motives might invalidate your worship?
?
The passage says tawhid is 'the best of obediences'. Is your pursuit of tawhid—learning about it, affirming it, and living it—your top priority? Or do you give more attention to other aspects of religion while neglecting this foundation? What one change can you make today to elevate tawhid in your life?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment with only your deeds. The passage states tawhid is the cause of salvation from eternal punishment. Do you truly believe that your salvation hinges on this single reality? If so, why do you not constantly reinvigorate your tawhid? What specific steps will you take this week to deepen your understanding and practice of tawhid?

Interpreting Scriptural Texts on Temporality and Affirming Divine Transcendence
The poet outlines three theological principles: interpretive method for huduth texts, negation of opposites, and affirmation of divine possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to interpret scriptural texts that seem to attribute temporality to God, why opposites are impossible for Him, and what kinds of actions are permissible for God.

Core Idea
Interpret huduth texts, deny opposites, and affirm divine possibility.

In this lesson, we explore three key principles from the poet's concise passage: how to handle scriptural texts that seem to imply temporality (huduth), the impossibility of opposites (such as spatial location) for God, and the scope of divine action. These principles safeguard God's transcendence and provide a clear method for understanding revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the three distinct rules the poet presents.
Notice the poetic rhythm; each rule is a separate clause.
2
Examine the rule on huduth texts
Focus on the first line: 'Every text that indicates temporality – carry it upon the wording that indicates [the intended meaning]'. This teaches that such texts must be interpreted (ta'wil) in a way consistent with God's eternal nature.
This is a hermeneutical principle, not a denial of scripture.
3
Understand the negation of opposites and divine possibility
The second line states that opposites (like spatial location) are impossible for God. The third line says that what is possible for created beings (e.g., giving or removing wealth) is permissible for God. Consider how these affirm God's transcendence and freedom.
Opposites here refer to attributes that would contradict God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكل نص للحدوث دلا احمل على اللفظ الذي قد دلا. ويستحيل ضد ذي الصفات في حقه كالكون في الجهات. وجائز في حقه ما أمكننا إيجادًا إعدامًا كرزقه الغنى.
And every text that indicates temporality (huduth) – carry it upon the wording that indicates [the intended meaning]. And it is impossible for the opposite of the attributes [to occur] in His right, like spatial location (kawn fi al-jahat). And permissible in His right is what is possible for us, in terms of creation or annihilation, like His providing wealth.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage provides hermeneutical rules. First, any scriptural text that seems to indicate that God is subject to temporal origination (huduth) must be interpreted (ta'wil) in a way consistent with His attributes. Second, it is impossible for God to have opposites of His attributes, such as being in a spatial direction (jihaat). Third, what is possible (ja'iz) for God is everything that is logically possible, such as bestowing wealth or taking it away—these are not necessary or impossible for Him.

Key Terms
الحدوث
تأويل
ضد
صفات
جهات
جائز
إيجاد
إعدام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that texts mentioning God's 'coming' or 'hands' must be taken literally, implying temporality or physicality.
Correction: Such texts are to be interpreted (ta'wil) in a way that befits God's transcendence, as the poet states. They do not imply temporality or bodily attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the poet say about texts that indicate huduth?
A) Take them literally
B) Reject them
C) Interpret them in line with the intended meaning
D) Ignore them

Key Takeaway
The poet gives a three-part safeguard: interpret huduth texts, deny opposites, and affirm divine possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Quranic verse that describes God in a way that seems to imply change or movement, do you feel a tension? How does the first rule of this lesson challenge you to submit your immediate reaction to a deeper understanding of God's transcendence? Today, what specific verse will you approach with this interpretive method?
?
The poet says it is impossible for God to have spatial location. Do you ever catch yourself subconsciously imagining God in a certain place, like above the heavens? How can you train your mind to affirm His transcendence without spatial limits? Write down one practical way you will remind yourself this week.
?
Consider the third rule: what is possible for us is permissible for God. Are you tempted to limit God's power based on your own experience of what is possible? For example, do you truly believe God can provide you with wealth even when your circumstances seem impossible? What would change in your dua if you internalized this principle?

God's Acts: Neither Coerced Nor Arbitrary
Al-Laqqani negates coercion and free choice for God, then affirms grace and justice in reward/punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's actions are not compelled by any external force nor subject to human-like free choice, and that His reward is by pure grace and punishment by pure justice.

Core Idea
Allah is not coerced, nor does He choose like us; His reward is grace, His punishment is justice.

Al-Laqqani begins by clearing the ground: we should not think of Allah's actions in human terms. He is not forced to act, nor does He deliberate like creatures. Then he establishes the foundation of His dealings: reward is pure favor, punishment is pure justice—nothing is owed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic lines carefully
Read the first two lines of the poem without rushing. Notice the rhythm and the key negations.
The Arabic uses a dense poetic style; each phrase carries theological weight.
2
Identify the two negations
The poet says Allah is not 'majburan' (coerced) and not acting by 'ikhtiyar' (free choice) in the human sense. Ask why he negates both extremes.
This sets Allah apart from both involuntary and voluntary human actions.
3
Understand the positive statements
Then he states that reward is by pure grace (fadl) and punishment by pure justice (adl). This means Allah is not obligated to either outcome.
Contrast with Mu'tazilite view that God must reward obedience and punish sin.
4
Reflect on the meaning of 'kullan'
The phrase 'wala kullan yaf'alu ikhtiyaran' might mean 'not everyone acts by choice'—a hint that human free will is limited.
This connects to Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلَيْسَ مَجْبورًا ولا اخْتِيَارَا وَلَيْسَ كُلًّا يَفْعَلُ اختيارًا فإنْ يَبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ وإنْ يُعَذِّبْ فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ وَقَولُهُمْ إِنَّ الصَّلاحَ وَاجِبُ عليه زُورٌ مَا عَلَيْهِ وَاجِبُ أَلَمْ يَرَوْا إِيْلَامَهُ الأطْفَالا وَشِبْهَهَا فَحَاذِرِ المِحَالَا وجائز عَليْه خَلْقُ الشَّرِّ والخير كالإسلامِ وجَهْلِ الكُفْرِ وَوَاجِبُ إِيمَانُنَـا بـالقَدَرِ وَبالقَضَا كَمَا أَتَى في الخَبَر
So He is not coerced, nor free choice? And not everyone acts by choice. If He rewards, it is by pure grace; and if He punishes, it is by pure justice. And their statement that what is right is obligatory upon Him is false; nothing is obligatory upon Him. Did they not see His causing pain to children and the like? So beware of falsehood. And it is permissible for Him to create evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of unbelief. And it is obligatory for us to believe in al-qadr (divine decree) and al-qada (divine ordainment) as it came in the report.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a series of theological claims about divine acts. The author begins by stating that God is neither compelled nor does He act arbitrarily—He is not 'coerced' (majburan) nor does He exercise 'free choice' (ikhtiyaran) in the human sense. The phrase 'not everyone acts by choice' may refer to the Ash'ari position that God's acts are not subject to external constraints. Next, the poet asserts that if God rewards, it is by pure grace (fadl), and if He punishes, it is by pure justice (adl). This counters the Mu'tazilite view that God must reward the obedient and punish the disobedient. Then the poet refutes the claim that it is obligatory (wajib) upon God to do what is most beneficial (al-salah); he calls such a statement false (zur). He provides an example: the suffering of children—if God harms them, it is not unjust because He owns creation absolutely. The poet warns against sophistry (mihal). Next, he states that God may create both evil (sharr) and good (khair), including Islam and unbelief—affirming that all acts are created by God, even those opposed to His command. Finally, the poet declares that belief in divine decree (al-qadr) and ordainment (al-qada) is obligatory (wajib), as transmitted in revelation (al-khabar).

Key Terms
مَجْبور
اخْتِيار
فَضْل
عَدْل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Allah's reward consist of?
A) It is an obligation upon Him.
B) It is by pure grace (fadl).
C) It is earned by the creature.
D) It is arbitrary.

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions are free from external compulsion and not analogous to human choice; reward is grace, punishment is justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that Allah owes you reward for your good deeds? How does this passage's teaching that reward is pure grace challenge that belief, and what change of heart does it demand from you?
?
When you face hardship or punishment in this life, do you see it as unjust? How does this passage realign your understanding of divine justice and your own accountability?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah's reward is pure grace and not a payment, would your worship and obedience feel more like a gift or a transaction? What would you need to change to live in that reality?

Refuting Obligatory Rightness on God
Al-Laqqani refutes the claim that God must do what is most beneficial, using the example of children's suffering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari rejection of the Mu'tazilite claim that God must do what is most beneficial (al-salah), and the evidence from the suffering of children.

Core Idea
Nothing is obligatory upon Allah; He does as He wills, and His actions are not subject to human notions of benefit.

The poet vigorously denies the claim that God is obligated to do what is best for creation. He calls it false (zur) and gives a powerful example: the suffering of innocent children. This teaches us that God's absolute ownership over creation means nothing is owed to creatures.

Guided Path
1
Read the third and fourth lines
These lines contain the refutation and the proof. Read them slowly.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to the negation 'maa 'alayhi waajib'.
2
Identify the false claim
The poet attributes to others the statement that 'al-salah' (what is right/most beneficial) is obligatory upon God.
This was a key Mu'tazilite doctrine.
3
Analyze the example of children's pain
The poet asks: did they not see that Allah inflicts pain on children? If God were obligated to do what is best, He would not allow such suffering.
This is a powerful counterexample.
4
Heed the warning
The line ends with 'fahadhir al-mihala' (beware of sophistry). Avoid twisting the plain meaning of the text.
This warns against rationalist distortion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلَيْسَ مَجْبورًا ولا اخْتِيَارَا وَلَيْسَ كُلًّا يَفْعَلُ اختيارًا فإنْ يَبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ وإنْ يُعَذِّبْ فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ وَقَولُهُمْ إِنَّ الصَّلاحَ وَاجِبُ عليه زُورٌ مَا عَلَيْهِ وَاجِبُ أَلَمْ يَرَوْا إِيْلَامَهُ الأطْفَالا وَشِبْهَهَا فَحَاذِرِ المِحَالَا وجائز عَليْه خَلْقُ الشَّرِّ والخير كالإسلامِ وجَهْلِ الكُفْرِ وَوَاجِبُ إِيمَانُنَـا بـالقَدَرِ وَبالقَضَا كَمَا أَتَى في الخَبَر
So He is not coerced, nor free choice? And not everyone acts by choice. If He rewards, it is by pure grace; and if He punishes, it is by pure justice. And their statement that what is right is obligatory upon Him is false; nothing is obligatory upon Him. Did they not see His causing pain to children and the like? So beware of falsehood. And it is permissible for Him to create evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of unbelief. And it is obligatory for us to believe in al-qadr (divine decree) and al-qada (divine ordainment) as it came in the report.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a series of theological claims about divine acts. The author begins by stating that God is neither compelled nor does He act arbitrarily—He is not 'coerced' (majburan) nor does He exercise 'free choice' (ikhtiyaran) in the human sense. The phrase 'not everyone acts by choice' may refer to the Ash'ari position that God's acts are not subject to external constraints. Next, the poet asserts that if God rewards, it is by pure grace (fadl), and if He punishes, it is by pure justice (adl). This counters the Mu'tazilite view that God must reward the obedient and punish the disobedient. Then the poet refutes the claim that it is obligatory (wajib) upon God to do what is most beneficial (al-salah); he calls such a statement false (zur). He provides an example: the suffering of children—if God harms them, it is not unjust because He owns creation absolutely. The poet warns against sophistry (mihal). Next, he states that God may create both evil (sharr) and good (khair), including Islam and unbelief—affirming that all acts are created by God, even those opposed to His command. Finally, the poet declares that belief in divine decree (al-qadr) and ordainment (al-qada) is obligatory (wajib), as transmitted in revelation (al-khabar).

Key Terms
صَلاح
زُور
مِحَال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God is obligated to do what is most beneficial (al-salah) for creation.
Correction: The passage clearly states this is false; nothing is obligatory upon God, as seen in the suffering of innocent children.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the statement that God must do what is most beneficial (al-salah)?
A) It is obligatory upon God.
B) It is false (zur).
C) It is permissible.
D) It is recommended.

Key Takeaway
Nothing is obligatory upon Allah; the fact that children suffer disproves the claim that God must always do what is most beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a tragedy — an illness, a loss, or an injustice — do you instinctively feel that God has wronged you or owes you an explanation? How does this passage challenge that feeling and call you to submit to His absolute freedom?
?
Have you ever quietly assumed that your good deeds obligate God to reward you or protect you from harm? In light of 'nothing is obligatory upon Him,' what would it take for you to truly let go of that expectation and worship Him without conditions?
?
You have just read that God causes pain to innocent children. Does this disturb you? If so, what does that disturbance reveal about your own assumptions about God? Will you now reject the falsehood that He must act according to your standard of fairness, and instead trust that He does as He wills for reasons you may never understand?

God Creates Good and Evil; Belief in Qadr
Al-Laqqani affirms that God creates good and evil, and commands belief in qadr and qada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah creates both good and evil, and that belief in divine decree (qadr) and ordainment (qada) is obligatory.

Core Idea
Allah creates all things, good and evil; we must believe in His decree as revealed.

The final lines address the scope of divine creation: God creates everything, including faith and disbelief. This does not mean He approves of evil. Then the poet concludes by stating the obligation to believe in qadr and qada, as taught in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the last two lines
These lines cover the creation of good and evil and the obligation of belief in qadr.
The Arabic is straightforward but theologically profound.
2
Note the permissibility of creating both good and evil
The poet says it is 'jaa'iz' (permissible) for God to create both good and evil, citing Islam and the ignorance of disbelief as examples.
This affirms that God is the creator of all actions, including those He commands and prohibits.
3
Understand the examples
The examples 'like Islam and the ignorance of unbelief' show that God creates both obedience and disobedience.
This does not mean He approves of disbelief; rather, He creates it as a test.
4
Grasp the obligation to believe in qadr and qada
The final line states that belief in divine decree and ordainment is obligatory, as it came in the revealed reports.
This ties back to the Quran and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلَيْسَ مَجْبورًا ولا اخْتِيَارَا وَلَيْسَ كُلًّا يَفْعَلُ اختيارًا فإنْ يَبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ وإنْ يُعَذِّبْ فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ وَقَولُهُمْ إِنَّ الصَّلاحَ وَاجِبُ عليه زُورٌ مَا عَلَيْهِ وَاجِبُ أَلَمْ يَرَوْا إِيْلَامَهُ الأطْفَالا وَشِبْهَهَا فَحَاذِرِ المِحَالَا وجائز عَليْه خَلْقُ الشَّرِّ والخير كالإسلامِ وجَهْلِ الكُفْرِ وَوَاجِبُ إِيمَانُنَـا بـالقَدَرِ وَبالقَضَا كَمَا أَتَى في الخَبَر
So He is not coerced, nor free choice? And not everyone acts by choice. If He rewards, it is by pure grace; and if He punishes, it is by pure justice. And it is permissible for Him to create evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of unbelief. Commentary

The author begins by stating that God is neither compelled nor does He act arbitrarily—He is not 'coerced' (majburan) nor does He exercise 'free choice' (ikhtiyaran) in the human sense. The phrase 'not everyone acts by choice' may refer to the Ash'ari position that God's acts are not subject to external constraints. According to the passage, what is the ruling on believing in al-qadr and al-qada?
A) It is recommended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a personal trial that feels evil—like illness or loss—do you truly believe it is created by Allah? How does that belief sit in your heart, and what would change if you fully surrendered to His decree without resentment?
?
Consider a sin you committed recently. You know Allah created that act, yet you are accountable for it. Does this tension make you despair of His mercy, or does it drive you to repent more sincerely and cling to His forgiveness?
?
If Allah creates both faith and disbelief, how does that affect your attitude toward non-believers? Does it lead you to arrogance (thinking you deserved faith) or to humility and gratitude, fearing that the same power could leave you in ignorance?

beginner
5 min
Defining Tawhid: Essence Beyond Resemblance and Negation
The commentator defines tawhid and explains its centrality in the prophetic mission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of tawhid as affirming an essence neither resembling others nor devoid of attributes, and why the Prophet was sent specifically with this message of tawhid.

Core Idea
Tawhid is the affirmation of God's unique essence with all attributes, avoiding both anthropomorphism and negation.

The commentator begins by defining the core concept of tawhid before explaining the line from the poem. He gives a definition that immediately guards against two dangerous extremes: likening God to creation and stripping Him of His attributes. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows why tawhid is the heart of the prophetic message.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the definition.
2
Understand the definition of tawhid
Identify the key phrase 'إثبات ذات غير مشبهة بالذوات، ولا معطلة عن الصفات'. This defines tawhid as affirming an essence that is neither similar to other essences nor stripped of attributes.
This guards against both tashbih (anthropomorphism) and ta'til (negation).
3
Grasp why tawhid is the noblest worship
The passage states tawhid is the noblest act of worship, best obedience, condition for validity, and cause of salvation. Reflect on why each of these points makes tawhid central.
Think about how tawhid underpins all other acts.
4
Analyze the linguistic interpretation of 'khala al-din'
The commentator explains the phrase as a circumstantial clause: the religion (of the people) was empty of tawhid when the Prophet came. Note the contrast between tawhid and multiple false gods.
This highlights the historical context of polytheism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الدين وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته. وقيل: التوحيد: إثبات ذات غير مشبهة بالذوات، ولا معطلة عن الصفات. وتخصيص الإرسال بالتوحيد؛ لأنه أشرف العبادات، وأفضل الطاعات، وشرط في صحتها، وسبب في النجاة من العذاب المخلد. وقد خلا الدين عن التوحيد أي تجرد عن التوحيد جملة حالية مقيدة لنبي، أي: جاء من عند الله بالتوحيد في حال تعدد المعبودات الباطلة وخلو الدين، أي فراغه عن التوحيد والتفرد.
Statement of the meaning of religion and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. And it was said: al-tawhid is affirming an essence not resembling essences, nor devoid of attributes. And specifying the sending with tawhid because it is the noblest of acts of worship, the best of acts of obedience, a condition for their validity, and a cause for salvation from eternal punishment. And indeed religion was devoid of tawhid, i.e., stripped of tawhid – a circumstantial clause qualifying 'for a prophet', meaning: he came from Allah with tawhid in a state of multiple false objects of worship and the emptiness of religion, i.e., its vacuity of tawhid and singularity.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines tawhid and explains the line 'wa qad khala al-din 'an al-tawhid' from the poem. First, the commentator provides a definition of tawhid: affirming an Essence that is neither similar to other essences nor stripped of attributes. This definition guards against two extremes: tashbih (anthropomorphism) and ta'til (negation of attributes). Then he explains that the prophet was sent specifically with tawhid because it is the most honorable act of worship, the best act of obedience, a condition for the validity of deeds, and the cause of salvation from eternal punishment. Next, he analyzes the phrase 'wa qad khala al-din 'an al-tawhid' as a circumstantial clause: the religion (i.e., the religious practices of the people before the prophet) was completely empty of tawhid at the time when the prophet came with tawhid, in a situation of multiple false gods. The word 'din' here is understood in the sense of the beliefs and practices of the people, which were devoid of monotheism.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawhid merely means believing in one God without any attributes.
Correction: The definition explicitly includes affirming attributes: 'nor devoid of attributes', showing that tawhid includes positive attributes.

Key Takeaway
Tawhid is the affirmation of God's unique essence with all attributes, and the Prophet was sent to establish it as the foundation of worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Prophet was sent specifically to establish tawhid because it is the condition for the validity of all your deeds. Is your prayer, your charity, your daily life built on this foundation? Or are you performing deeds that were never accepted because you haven't purified your tawhid from hidden shirk?
?
Salvation from eternal punishment depends on tawhid. Do you live with that urgency? Or have you reduced tawhid to a mere intellectual acceptance while your heart is attached to people, status, or comfort? What one attachment are you unwilling to surrender for the sake of pure tawhid?

The Attribute of Mukhalafa li al-Hawadith – Meaning and Proof
Definition of the attribute of dissimilarity and its connection to the proof from eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the attribute of dissimilarity to created things and why its proof is linked to Allah's eternity.

Core Idea
Allah is unlike anything; the proof is the same as His eternity.

This lesson introduces the third negative attribute: al-mukhalafa li al-hawadith (dissimilarity to contingent things). The author states that its proof is the same as the proof for Allah's eternity. We will explore what this attribute means and how it protects against anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the author introduces the attribute and immediately connects it to the proof of eternity.
The text is incomplete; the full explanation of the meaning is cut off.

The author says the discussion covers two points: (1) the meaning of dissimilarity, (2) the proof that it is necessary. The available text only gives the beginning of the first point.
3
Understand the meaning given so far
The meaning as stated is that nothing among contingent things resembles Allah, neither in His essence (the text cuts off here). Reflect on what this implies about Allah's transcendence.
This attribute guards against any form of anthropomorphism.
4
Connect the proof to eternity
The poetic line states 'the proof of this is eternity.' This means that the proof for Allah's dissimilarity is the same as the proof for His eternity. Ask yourself: why does eternity guarantee dissimilarity?
Contingent things are temporal; the Eternal cannot resemble them.

The argument likely continues that since Allah is eternal and all contingent things are temporal, they cannot resemble Him. This attribute guards against anthropomorphism.

The Meaning of Mukhalafah lil Hawadith
Al-Iji defines mukhalafah and its implications negating jirmiyyah and 'ardiyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the attribute 'mukhalafah lil hawadith' (being unlike created things) and how it requires negating corporeality and accidentalness from Allah.

Core Idea
Allah is unlike everything — in essence, attributes, and actions — negating body and accident.

In this passage, al-Iji defines the crucial attribute of tanzih: that Allah is utterly unlike any created thing. He explains that this unlikeness covers essence, attributes, and actions, and has concrete implications: it negates from Allah two categories of creaturely imperfections — corporeality (jirmiyyah) and accidentalness ('ardiyyah) — along with their necessary attachments. Understanding this definition is foundational for grasping Islamic theology's insistence on divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opening division
Notice that al-Iji divides the discussion into two: the meaning and the proof. This lesson covers only the meaning. Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall statement.
2
Grasp the core negation
The meaning is that no creature resembles Allah in essence, attributes, or actions. This foundational point is the heart of tanzih.
Mukhalafah covers all three domains: essence, attributes, actions.
3
Explore the negated categories
From this unlikeness, two categories are negated: corporeality (jirmiyyah) and accidentalness ('ardiyyah). Each has four attachments. Identify and understand each attachment.
Corporeality attachments: composition, spatial occupancy, temporal origination, receptivity to accidents. Accidentalness attachments: temporal origination, not self-subsisting, need for another, annihilation in second moment.
4
Summarize and conclude
Combine the details to articulate the full meaning: Allah's essence, attributes, and actions are utterly unique. This lesson establishes the negative aspect of divine transcendence.
This prepares for the next lesson on the proof of this attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على صفة المخالفة للحوادث في موضعين ، الأول: معنى المخالفة للحوادث ، والثاني: بيان الدليل على وجوب مخالفته للحوادث. أما الموضع الأول: فإن معنى مخالفته للحوادث أن شيئًا منها لا يماثله سبحانه لا في ذاته ولا في صفاته ولا في أفعاله، وهذه المخالفة تنفي عنه له الجرمية والعرضية ولوازم كل منهما ، فأما لوازم الجرمية ، فأربعة ، وهي التركيب والتحيز ، والحدوث ، وقبول الأعراض: كالمقادير ، والجهات والأزمنة والقُرب، والبعد، والمماسة، والحركة، والسكون، والصغر ، والكبر، والطول ، والقصر ، وأما لوازم العرضية فأربعة أيضا: الحدوث ، وعدم قيامه بنفسه ، ووجوب قيامه بغيره، وانعدامه في الزمان الثاني ، بناءً على القول بأن العرض لا يبقى زمانين، وبالجملة المراد من مخالفته تعالى للحوادث أنَّ ذاته ليست كذات شيء من المخلوقات، وأن كل صفة من صفاته ليست كصفة شيء من المخلوقات، وأن أفعاله ليست كأفعال المخلوقات.
The discussion on the attribute of being unlike created things (mukhalafah lil hawadith) is in two places: first, the meaning of being unlike created things, and second, the explanation of the proof for the necessity of His being unlike created things. As for the first place, the meaning of His being unlike created things is that no created thing resembles Him, may He be exalted, not in His essence, nor in His attributes, nor in His actions. This unlikeness negates from Him corporeality (jirmiyyah) and accidentalness ('ardiyyah) and the attachments of each. As for the attachments of corporeality, they are four: composition (tarkib), spatial occupancy (tahayyuz), temporal origination (huduth), and receptivity to accidents (qubul al-a'rad) such as measures, directions, times, nearness, distance, contact, motion, stillness, smallness, largeness, length, and shortness. As for the attachments of accidentalness, they are also four: temporal origination, not subsisting by itself (qiyam bi-nafsihi), necessity of subsisting through another (wujub qiyamihi bi-ghayrihi), and its annihilation in a second moment (in'idamuhu fi al-zaman al-thani), based on the saying that an accident does not persist for two moments. In summary, the intended meaning of His being unlike created things is that His essence is not like the essence of any creature, every attribute of His is not like the attribute of any creature, and His actions are not like the actions of creatures.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of mukhalafah lil hawadith (being unlike created things) and explains its implications. It begins by stating that the discussion has two parts: the meaning in the first part, and the proof in the second. The first part is given here: the meaning is that nothing from created things resembles God in His essence, attributes, or actions. This unlikeness requires negating from God two categories: corporeality (jirmiyyah) and accidentalness ('ardiyyah), each with their attachments. The attachments of corporeality are listed as four: composition (i.e., being made of parts), spatial occupancy (occupying a location), temporal origination (having a beginning in time), and receptivity to accidents (being subject to changes like size, direction, time, proximity, distance, contact, motion, stillness, etc.). The attachments of accidentalness are also four: temporal origination (an accident is new), not subsisting by itself (an accident depends on a substance), the necessity of subsisting through another (it cannot exist independently), and its cessation in a second moment (accidents do not persist). The passage concludes by summarizing the meaning: God's essence, attributes, and actions are not like those of any creature.

قبول الأعراض
عدم قيامه بنفسه
وجوب قيامه بغيره
انعدامه في الزمان الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mukhalafah only applies to Allah's essence, not to His attributes or actions.
Correction: The text explicitly states that Mukhalafah covers essence, attributes, and actions: 'no created thing resembles Him ... not in His essence, nor in His attributes, nor in His actions.'

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The attribute of mukhalafah means Allah is unlike any creature in essence, attributes, and actions, which requires denying Him corporeality and accidentalness and their attachments.

?

Proof of Allah's Eternity from His Dissimilarity to Originated Things
Al-Bajuri defines mukhālafa and provides a proof of qidam from the classification of originated things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mukhālafa and the rational proof that Allah's dissimilarity to originated things establishes His eternity.

Core Idea
Allah's dissimilarity to originated things is the proof that He is eternal.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining what it means for Allah to be unlike originated things. He then grounds this definition in a rational proof based on the categories of created entities. This lesson unpacks both the definition and the logical argument that establishes Allah's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style: the definition is given first, then the proof.
2
Understand the definition of mukhālafa
Identify the key elements being negated from Allah: corporeality, accidentality, universality, particularity, and their necessities.
This sets the stage for the proof.
3
Identify the classification of originated things
Find the three categories: bodies (ajsām), substances (jawāhir), and accidents (aʿrāḍ). Note that accidents further include times, places, directions, and limits.
This classification is the foundation of the proof.
4
Follow the proof that none can be eternal
Trace the syllogism: all originated things are in these categories; none is necessary of existence because they are originated; therefore none is eternal. Then the conclusion: Allah, who is dissimilar, is eternal.
The proof is not circular; it relies on the fact that originated things cannot be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخالفة للحوادث واللاحقة كالنِّعم الأخرويَّة، والمخالفة لما ذُكر: عبارة عن سَلْبِ الجِرمية والعرضيّة أو الكلّيّة والجزئيّة ولوازمهما عنه تعالى. وإنَّما وجبَ له تعالى ما ذُكِر ؛ لأنَّ الحوادث إما أجسام، وإما جواهر، وإمَّا أعراض، والأعراض إما أزمنة وإما أمكنة وإما جهات وإما حدود ونهايات، ولا شيء منها بواجب الوجود؛ لما ثبت لها من الحدوث واستحالة القدم عليها، برهان هذا الحكم الواجب له تعالى وهو مخالفته للحوادث القدَم أي هو دليل ثبوت القِدم له؛ لأنَّ كلَّ ما وجبَ له القدم بالمعنى السابق استحال عليه العَدمُ، ولا شيء من الحوادث بمستحيل عليه العدم؛ فلا شيء منها بقديم.
Dissimilarity to originated things and their concomitants like the hereafter blessings, and the dissimilarity to what was mentioned: it is expression of negating corporeality, accidentiality, or universality and particularity and their necessities from Him, exalted. And indeed it is necessary for Him, exalted, what was mentioned because originated things are either bodies, or substances, or accidents; and accidents are either times, or places, or directions, or limits and ends. And none of them is necessary of existence due to their having originatedness and the impossibility of eternality upon them. The proof of this judgment necessary for Him, exalted, i.e., His dissimilarity to originated things, is eternality, i.e., it is the proof of the establishment of eternality for Him because everything for which eternality is necessary in the previous sense, non-existence becomes impossible for it, and none of the originated things is impossible for non-existence, so none of them is eternal.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a definition followed by a proof. Al-Bajuri first defines 'mukhālafa li al-ḥawādith' (dissimilarity to originated things) as the negation of corporeality (jirmiyya), accidentiality (ʿarḍiyya), universality (kulliyya), particularity (juzʾiyya), and their necessities from Allah. He then explains that this negation is necessary because all originated things (al-ḥawādith) fall into three categories: bodies (ajsām), substances (jawāhir), or accidents (aʿrāḍ). Accidents further include times, places, directions, and limits. None of these is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) because they are all originated (ḥādith) and cannot be eternal (qadīm). He then presents a proof that eternity (qidam) is an attribute of Allah: whatever is eternal (in the sense of having no beginning) cannot cease to exist, and all originated things can cease to exist, so none of them is eternal. This syllogism establishes that Allah is not a body, substance, or accident, and He is eternal.

Key Terms
مخالفة للحوادث
قديم
برهان
جسم
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes Allah is eternal to prove He is dissimilar.
Correction: The proof is not circular. It first classifies all originated things and shows they cannot be eternal because they are originated. Then, since Allah is dissimilar to them (as they are bodies, substances, accidents), He is eternal. The dissimilarity is not assumed but derived from the negation of those categories.

Checkpoint
Why must Allah be dissimilar to originated things according to Al-Bajuri's argument?
A) Because originated things are created by Allah.
B) Because originated things are either bodies, substances, or accidents, and none of these can be eternal.
C) Because Allah has no attributes.
D) Because originated things are multiple.

Key Takeaway
Allah is eternal because He is entirely dissimilar to originated things, none of which can be eternal due to their contingent nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that everything originated—your own body, your house, your family—is non-eternal and will cease to exist. Allah alone is eternal. If you truly believed this, how would it change the way you cling to worldly things today? Name one attachment you need to loosen, and a concrete step you will take this week to prioritize the Eternal over the transient.
?
The proof in this lesson shows that Allah is completely dissimilar to originated things. Yet you often imagine Allah in human terms or think of Him as like a powerful king. In what specific situation today have you subconsciously compared Allah to a created being? What can you do to train your mind to think of His absolute uniqueness?
?
If the proof that Allah is eternal is so clear from the nature of originated things, why do you sometimes live as if this world will never end? What is the one fear keeping you from fully surrendering to the Eternal? Commit to praying about that fear tonight and trusting Allah's eternal plan over your temporary concerns.

Threefold Unity: Essence, Attributes, and Actions
Sanusi explains the derivation of unity from divine attributes and points to later sections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various meanings of tawḥīd as they relate to Allah's essence, attributes, and actions, and how they are derived from the attribute of being dissimilar to contingent things.

Core Idea
Unity of essence, attributes, and actions—each rooted in divine uniqueness.

In this passage, Sanusi builds on the concept of divine unity by unpacking its specific meanings. He shows how the attribute of dissimilarity to creation (mukhālafat li-l-ḥawādith) implies unity of essence, and then distinguishes unity of attributes and unity of actions, pointing ahead to later discussions. He also notes two further aspects of unity that go beyond the immediate context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Let the author's structure become clear.
Pay special attention to the three kinds of unity defined.
2
Identify the three kinds of unity
The passage presents unity of essence, unity of attributes, and unity of actions. Note how each is defined.
Unity of essence is derived from the attribute of dissimilarity to contingents.
3
Understand the derivation from attributes
Sanusi says unity of essence is 'derived from the attribute of dissimilarity to contingent things'. This means that because Allah is unlike creation, He cannot be composed of parts.
4
Note the two additional meanings
Sanusi mentions two more aspects of unity not covered here: that there is no essence like Allah's essence, and no attribute like His attribute.
These are reserved for later sections of the poem.
5
Reflect on the comprehensive nature of unity
Consider how these five aspects together give a full picture of tawḥīd: no composition, no similar attributes, no partner in actions, and no likeness in essence or attributes.
This shows the depth of Islamic monotheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ وحدة الذات بمعنى: عدم التركيب من أجزاء مستفادة من صفة المخالفة للحوادث ، وأن وحدة الصفات بمعنى: أنه ليس له سبحانه صفتان من جنس واحد قد أفردها المصنف بالذكر فيما يأتي حيث قال: (وَوَحْدَةٌ أَوْجِبْ لَهَا ... إلخ) وأن وحدة الأفعال بمعنى: أنه ليس لغيره سبحانه فعل مِن الأفعال على وجه الإيجاد، قد أفردها المصنف أيضا بالذكر فيما يأتي في مبحث خلق الأفعال حيث قال : وخَالقٌ لعبدِه ومَا عَمِل ... إلخ) فبقي مما تدل عليه الوحدانية ولم يذكر في غير هذا الموضع أمران : وحدة الذات، بمعنى: أنه ليس هناك ذات أخرى تشبه ذاته ، ووحدة الصفات، بمعنى أنه ليس لغيره صفة تشبه صفته .
And know that the unity of the essence means: the absence of composition from parts, derived from the attribute of being different from contingent things. And the unity of the attributes means: that He, glorified is He, does not have two attributes of the same kind—this the author (al-muṣannif) has singled out for mention later where he said: 'And the unity oblige for her... etc.' And the unity of actions means: that no one other than He, glorified is He, has any action in the manner of bringing into existence (ījād); this the author has also singled out for mention later in the topic of the creation of actions where he said: 'And Creator of His servant and what he did... etc.' So there remain, from what al-wahdāniyya indicates and is not mentioned in this place, two matters: the unity of the essence, meaning that there is no essence resembling His essence, and the unity of the attributes, meaning that no one has an attribute resembling His attribute.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage provides further clarification on the meanings of unity of essence, attributes, and actions. The unity of essence is knowledge from the attribute of being dissimilar to contingents. The unity of attributes negates having two attributes of the same kind, and the author will discuss this later. The unity of actions negates any other creator, also discussed later. The author notes two additional aspects not covered here: the unity of essence in the sense of having no similar essence, and the unity of attributes in the sense of having no similar attribute. This ties the discussion to other parts of the work.

Correction: Sanusi clarifies that it also includes the absence of any essence similar to Allah's, as he states 'unity of the essence, meaning that there is no essence resembling His essence'.

Key Takeaway
Divine unity encompasses not only the absence of composition but also the uniqueness of essence, attributes, and actions, with no partner or likeness.

Negating Opposition from God
Commentator explains the negation of an opposite from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for why God cannot have any true opposite or opponent.

Core Idea
An eternal opposite would force God to cease, which is impossible.

The poet and commentator address an essential aspect of divine transcendence (tanzih): God is absolutely free from any opponent or opposite. This lesson unpacks the logical reasoning behind that negation, showing how the existence of a 'didd' would contradict God's necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the matn line and the commentator's explanation together. Notice how the commentator connects the negation to a conditional argument.
The matn line is concise; the commentary expands it.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'ضد' (opponent), 'مضاد' (opposite), 'ارتفاع' (disappearance), and 'خلف' (absurd). Understand their roles in the argument.
3
Analyze the conditional logic
The commentator states that if there were an opponent, God or His attributes would have to disappear. Consider the two cases: opponent eternal (permanent disappearance) or temporary (disappearance during its existence).
The argument relies on the impossibility of the Necessary Being ceasing to exist.
4
Reach the conclusion
Since God is wājib al-wujūd (Necessary Existent) and eternal, any scenario requiring His disappearance is absurd (khulf). Therefore, no opponent can exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَوَالِدٍ كَذَا الوَلَدْ والأَصْدِقَا - عَنْ ضِدُّ أَوْ شِبْهِ شَرِيكَ مُطْلَقَا
وعلق بقوله منزها ( عَن ضِدَّ) أي : مُضاد له أو الصفاته ، وإلا لوجب ارتفاعه (٢) أو ارتفاعها ارتفاعاً مطلقا إنْ دامَ الضّدُّ، أو مُقيَّداً بحالة وجودِه إن لم يَدُم ، والفَرْضُ أنَّه واجب الوجود قديمٌ ، وكذا صفاته، هذا خُلْفٌ.
And a parent, likewise a child, and the most truthful, from an opponent (didd) or a partner absolutely. And he commented on his statement 'transcendent' (from an opponent): meaning a contradictory to Him or to His attributes, otherwise His disappearance or their disappearance would be necessary, absolutely if the opponent persists, or conditionally upon its existence if it does not persist, and the assumption is that He is necessary of existence, eternal, and likewise His attributes. This is absurd.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage includes a line from the matn (poem) that negates any 'didd' (opponent or opposite) from God. The commentator explains the phrase 'from an opponent' in the matn: it means anything that is in opposition to God Himself or to His attributes. The reasoning is that if such an opponent existed, it would necessitate the disappearance of God or His attributes. If the opponent is eternal, then God would have to cease to exist permanently; if the opponent is temporary, God would cease during its existence. But God is necessarily existent and eternal, so this is impossible. Therefore, having an opposite is absurd (khulf). This is a standard tanzih (transcendence) argument: God cannot have a true opposite, because that would imply dependency or limitation.

Key Terms
ضد
مضاد
ارتفاع
واجب الوجود
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'ضد' (didd) refers to any different thing, so God has 'opposites' like light/darkness.
Correction: In kalām, 'didd' specifically means an opposite that could counteract God's existence or attributes, not mere difference.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what would be the consequence if God had a true opposite?
A) God would become superior.
B) God would be limited in space.
C) God or His attributes would have to disappear, which contradicts His necessity.
D) God would create the opposite.

Key Takeaway
God cannot have a true opposite because it would require His non-existence, contradicting His necessary and eternal nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever lived as if something or someone could really oppose God and hinder His will? When you fear a person, a loss, or a setback more than you fear God, you are treating that thing as a 'ضد' (opponent) that can overpower Him. This lesson declares that impossible. Will you repent of that hidden shirk and realign your heart to fear God alone?
?
The proof shows that if God had an opponent, even temporarily, He would cease to exist. Do you sometimes act as if your sins can temporarily 'cancel' God's mercy or power over your life? That would imply a temporary defeat of His attributes. This is khulf (absurd). Trust today that no sin, no circumstance, no force can ever oppose God's mercy toward you. Will you accept this and stop viewing your failures as obstacles to His grace?
?
This short but profound argument uproots any thought that something could be equal to God or stand against Him. Is there any assumption or doubt in your mind that treats something—wealth, health, a relationship, a fear—as if it could successfully oppose God's decree? Identify it now. Commit to memorizing the line 'عَن ضِدَّ أَوْ شِبْهِ شَرِيكَ مُطْلَقَا' and meditate on it until that false belief is uprooted.

Negation of Resemblance and the Three Degrees of Similarity
Commentary negating shabah and distinguishing three similarity terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'shabah' as negated from Allah and the distinction between shabah, nazir, and mithl based on the degree of attribute agreement.

Core Idea
Even the slightest resemblance to Allah is impossible; three terms quantify decreasing similarity.

This lesson explores the second term negated from Allah in the matn: shibh (similitude). The commentator clarifies its linguistic form and then answers a crucial question about the difference between three related terms: shabih, nazir, and mathil. By understanding their degrees of agreement, we grasp why even minimal resemblance is negated.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument emerge naturally.
Notice the poetic license in using 'or' instead of 'and'.
2
Understand the linguistic note
The commentator explains that words like shabah and shabih are variants with the same meaning. Note the pattern of shabah/ shabih and similar pairs.
3
Grasp the three degrees of agreement
The passage defines mithl (agreement in all attributes), nazir (agreement in most), and shabah (agreement in fewer). Understand each definition precisely.
These are not synonyms but a spectrum of similarity.
4
Reflect on the author's word choice
Ask yourself why the author chose 'shabah' (the weakest degree) rather than 'nazir' or 'mithl'. The commentary reveals the secret: even the slightest resemblance is negated.
This underscores absolute tanzih.

(أَوْ شِبْهِ) (۳) أي : مُشَابِهِ (٤) له تعالى في ذاته أو في صفاته بوجه وحال ؛
-
(3) قوله: (أو شبه) معطوف على (ضد) فأو بمعنى الواو ، عَدلَ إليه الناظم لضرورة النظم. الشنواني .
(4) الشبه - بكسر الشين وسكون الباء - مثل الشَّبيه ، وقد جاءت ألفاظ كثيرة في العربية على هاتين الزنتين ومعناهما واحد مثل : الخِدْن والخَدِين ، والخِلُّ والخَلِيل ، والمِثْل والمثيل، والشّبه والشَّبيه ، والحِبُّ والحَبِيب .

(Or a similitude (shibh)) (3) meaning: resembling Him, the Most High, in His essence or in His attributes in any aspect or state. (3) His statement: (or a similitude) is coordinated with (an opponent), so 'or' has the meaning of 'and'; the poet adopted it due to poetic necessity. Al-Shanwani. (4) Al-shabah (with kasra on shin and sukun on ba') is like al-shabih, and many words in Arabic appear on these two patterns with the same meaning, such as: al-khidn and al-khadeen, al-khill and al-khalil, al-mithl and al-mathil, al-shabah and al-shabih, al-hibb and al-habib. If you say: Is there a difference in meaning between al-shabih, al-nazir, and al-mathil? And if so, what is the limit of this difference? The answer is: We say to you: A thing's agreement with another thing is either in all its attributes, or in most of its attributes, or in fewer of its attributes. If the thing agrees with the other in all its attributes, then it is its mithl and mathil. If it agrees in most of its attributes, then it is its nazir. And if it agrees in fewer of its attributes, then it is its shabah. From here you understand the secret in why the author chose the word 'al-shabah' instead of 'al-nazir'.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage explains the second term negated from God: 'shibh' or 'shabah' (similitude). The commentator first notes that 'shibh' is coordinated with 'didd' and that the poet used 'or' instead of 'and' due to poetic meter. He then explains that 'shabah' means resembling God in essence or attributes in any aspect. The core of the lesson is a detailed linguistic and theological distinction between three terms: 'mithl/mathil' (like), 'nazir' (peer), and 'shabah' (resemblance). The commentator defines them by the degree of agreement: mithl agrees in all attributes, nazir agrees in most, and shabah agrees in fewer. The author of the matn deliberately chose 'shabah' (the least degree) because even minimal similarity is impossible for God. This underlines the absolute uniqueness of God in every respect, reinforcing tanzih.

Misconception: One might think that 'shabah' and 'nazir' are synonyms or that any similarity to Allah can be entertained.
Correction: The passage shows they differ in degree: shabah is the weakest (fewer attributes), nazir is stronger (most attributes). Even the weakest is negated from Allah, so no degree is permissible.

Proof of God's Transcendence from an Opposite
Proves the impossibility of an opposite to God through necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God cannot have an opposite, based on His necessary and eternal existence.

Core Idea
An opposite to God would require His possible non-existence — impossible for the Necessary Being.

This lesson examines a concise proof from Sanusi's text showing why God cannot have an opposite. Using a reductio ad absurdum, the argument demonstrates that any assumed opposite would force either the absolute or conditional termination of God's existence, which contradicts His attribute of necessary existence. Understanding this proof strengthens one's conviction in divine uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style; each phrase carries logical weight.
2
Identify the two conditional scenarios
Look for the contrasting conditions: if the opposite persists forever vs. if it does not. See what each implies for God's existence.
The phrase 'إن دام' and 'إن لم يدُم' mark the two branches.
3
See how they lead to a contradiction
Both scenarios result in the possibility of God ceasing to exist. But the text states that God is necessarily existent and eternal. This is the contradiction (khulf).
Understand that impossibility follows from the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلق بقوله منزّها ( عَن ضِدَّ) أي : مُضاد له أو الصفاته ، وإلا لوجب ارتفاعه (٢) أو ارتفاعها ارتفاعاً مطلقا إنْ دامَ الضّدُّ، أو مُقيَّداً بحالة وجودِه إن لم يَدُم ، والفَرْضُ أَنَّه واجب الوجود قديمٌ ، وكذا صفاته، هذا خُلْفٌ.
(2) قوله: (لوجب ارتفاعه أي بالفعل إن ثبت الضد بالفعل، أو جاز ارتفاعه إن جاز الضد. الأمير.
And he commented by his saying: 'Transcendent from (opposite)' meaning: one who opposes Him or His attributes, for otherwise His elevation would be necessary (2) or their elevation absolutely if the opposite persists, or conditionally upon the state of its existence if it does not persist, and the assumption is that He is necessarily existent, eternal, and likewise His attributes; this is a contradiction. (2) His saying: (for His elevation would be necessary) meaning: actually if the opposite is actually established, or its elevation would be possible if the opposite is possible. Al-Amīr.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction for the impossibility of an opposite to God. The commentator explains that if there were an opposite to God or His attributes, then either God would be elevated (i.e., cease to exist) absolutely if the opposite persists, or conditionally if the opposite does not persist. But since God is necessarily existent and eternal, His ceasing to exist is impossible. Therefore, the assumption of an opposite leads to a contradiction (khulf). The footnote clarifies the two possibilities: if the opposite is actual, then God's non-existence would be actual; if the opposite is only possible, then God's non-existence would be possible, but this contradicts His necessity.

Key Terms
ضد
مضاد
ارتفاع
واجب الوجود
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'opposite' means merely a contrary attribute, but here it means a separate entity that could coexist and necessitate God's non-existence.
Correction: The proof shows that any entity that could cause God's non-existence is impossible. The term 'opposite' in this context refers to a rival being that would contradict God's necessary existence.

Checkpoint

A) God would be limited in power
B) God would cease to exist either absolutely or conditionally
C) God would become composite
D) God's attributes would be contingent

Key Takeaway
God's necessary existence logically precludes any opposite that could cause His non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Understanding Shabah: The Minimal Likeness Negated from Allah
Explains the negation of likeness and distinguishes shabah, nazir, and mithl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical meaning of shabah in Islamic theology and how its negation implies the negation of stronger forms of likeness (nazir and mithl).

Core Idea
Negating the lowest degree of likeness covers all higher degrees.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid examines the term 'shabah' used in the creedal poem. The author explains that shabah refers to minimal resemblance in essence or attributes. Through a linguistic analysis of similarity terms, we learn why the poet chose 'shabah' over other words: because denying the weakest resemblance automatically denies any stronger resemblance. This precise terminology safeguards God's absolute uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice how the commentary defines 'shabah' and then distinguishes it from 'nazir' and 'mithl'.
2
Identify the three levels of similarity
The commentary presents three categories: shabah (minimal agreement), nazir (majority agreement), and mithl (complete agreement). Find the definitions for each.
The Arabic terms are shabah, nazir, mithl/mathil.
3
Understand the logical implication
The commentator argues that negating shabah implies negating nazir and mithl by primary implication. Reflect on why this is true: if the least similarity is impossible, then greater similarity is even more impossible.
This is a logical argument from priority (awla).
4
Consider the poetic choice
Ask yourself why the poet chose 'shabah' over 'nazir' or 'mithl'. The answer lies in the efficiency of negation: using the weakest term covers all stronger forms.
This shows the depth of the poet's theological precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(أَوْ شِبْهِ) (۳) أي : مُشَابِهِ (٤) له تعالى في ذاته أو في صفاته بوجه وحال ;
(3) قوله: (أو شبه) معطوف على (ضد) فأو بمعنى الواو ، عَدلَ إليه الناظم لضرورة النظم. الشنواني .
(4) الشبه - بكسر الشين وسكون الباء - مثل الشَّبيه ، وقد جاءت ألفاظ كثيرة في العربية على هاتين الزنتين ومعناهما واحد مثل : الخِدْن والخَدِين ، والخِلُّ والخَلِيل ، والمِثْل والمثيل، والشّبه والشَّبيه ، والحِبُّ والحَبِيب .
فإن قلت : فهل بين الشَّبيه والنظير والمثيل تفاوت في المعنى ، وإذا كان فما حد هذا التفاوت؟ فالجواب أن نقول لك: الشَّيء إن وافق الشَّيءَ فإمَّا أنْ يوافقه في كُلِّ صفاته، وإما أن يوافقه أغلب صفاته ، وإما أن يوافقه في أقل صفاته، فإن كان الشيء موافقا للشيء في كل صفاته فهو : مثله ومثيله، وإن كان يوافقه في أغلب صفاته: فهو نظيره، وإذا كان يوافقه في أقل صفاته فهو شبهه، ومن هنا تفهم السِّرَّ في اختيار المصنف لفظ «الشَّبْه» دون «النَّظير» و «المثل)) لأنَّ الحكم بنفي الشّبه يستلزم الحكم بنفي النَّظير والمثل ، فإنه إذا انتفى من يوافقه في أقل صفاته فَلَأَنْ ينتفي من يوافقه في أكثرها أو في جميعها من باب الأولى. محي.
(Or likeness) (3) meaning: one who resembles (4) Him the Exalted in His essence or in His attributes in any aspect or condition; (3) His saying: (or likeness) is conjoined to (opposite), so 'or' has the meaning of 'and', the poet switched to it due to poetic necessity. Ash-Shanwānī. (4) Ash-shabah (with kasr of shīn and sukūn of bā') is like ash-shabīh, and many words in Arabic have these two patterns with the same meaning, such as al-khidn and al-khadīn, al-khill and al-khalīl, al-mithl and al-mathīl, ash-shabah and ash-shabīh, al-ḥibb and al-ḥabīb. If you say: Is there a difference in meaning between ash-shabīh, an-naẓīr, and al-mathīl? And if so, what is the extent of this difference? The answer is to say to you: When a thing agrees with another, it either agrees in all its attributes, or in most of its attributes, or in least of its attributes. If the thing agrees with the thing in all its attributes, then it is its mithl and mathīl. If it agrees in most of its attributes, then it is its naẓīr. If it agrees in least of its attributes, then it is its shabah. From this you understand the secret in the author's choice of the word 'ash-shabah' over 'an-naẓīr' and 'al-mithl', because the judgment negating shabah entails the judgment negating naẓīr and mithl, for if that which agrees in least of its attributes is negated, then it is more fitting that what agrees in most or all of them is negated by primary implication. Muḥyī.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a definition and linguistic analysis of the term 'shabah' (likeness) as negated from God. The commentator first notes that 'shabah' means resembling in essence or attributes in any way. The footnote clarifies that 'or' here means 'and' due to poetic license. Then a detailed discussion distinguishes three levels of similarity: shabah (agreement in few attributes), naẓīr (agreement in most), and mithl/mathīl (agreement in all). Since the poem negates shabah, the commentator explains that this implicitly negates the higher degrees (naẓīr and mithl) by priority. The choice of 'shabah' is strategic: if the least similarity is impossible, the stronger ones are even more impossible.

Key Terms
شبه
شبيه
نظير
مثل
مثيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shabah means any kind of resemblance, so negating it only removes a weak form of similarity.
Correction: Shabah is the least degree of similarity; negating it automatically removes stronger degrees (nazir and mithl) by logical priority.

Checkpoint
Why did the poet choose the word 'shabah' (likeness) rather than 'nazir' or 'mithl' when negating resemblance to God?
A) Because 'shabah' is the strongest form of resemblance
B) Because negating shabah automatically negates stronger forms of resemblance
C) Because 'shabah' is easier to rhyme
D) Because 'shabah' implies equality in essence

Key Takeaway
Negating the least degree of resemblance (shabah) from Allah is sufficient to deny any stronger resemblance by logical priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself forming a mental image of Allah based on human traits, even subtly? How does the negation of the least resemblance (shabah) expose this as a violation of tawhid? What will you actively do to purge such thoughts from your heart today?
?
The author argues that negating the weakest form of resemblance covers all stronger forms. In your own spiritual life, are you neglecting small sins or minor lapses in 'aqeedah while thinking the major ones are enough to guard against? Where do you need to apply this principle of logical priority?
?
If the negation of shabah is sufficient to negate all likening, why do you still find yourself comfortable with vague comparisons of Allah to creation in your daily thoughts? What concrete step will you take this week to ensure your understanding of Allah is absolutely free from any trace of tashbih?

Transcendence from a Partner
States the negation of a partner in essence, attributes, and acts, citing the proof of oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three dimensions of God's transcendence from any partner and how this is grounded in the principle of oneness.

Core Idea
God is absolutely unique—no partner in essence, attributes, or actions.

This lesson focuses on a central claim of Islamic theology: God has no partner. The passage clarifies that 'partner' means any sharing in essence, attributes, or actions, and refers to the proof already established from the necessity of God's oneness. A footnote also offers a subtle critique of the phrasing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the direct and emphatic negation of any partner.
The passage uses the word 'mutaṭlaqan' (absolutely) to cover all aspects.
2
Identify the three aspects of transcendence
The text specifies three categories: essence, attributes, and actions. Find the Arabic terms for each and understand what is negated in each.
'Fala takthura fī dhātih' negates multiplicity in essence; 'lā naẓīra lahu fī ṣifātih' negates an equal in attributes; 'lā ikhtirā‘a li-ghayrihī fī af‘ālih' negates independent creation by another.
3
Reflect on the proof
The passage says the proof is 'what has passed concerning the necessity of oneness.' Consider why oneness (tawḥīd) logically excludes any partner. Connect this to the earlier discussion of God's necessary existence.
The proof for no partner in actions is noted as not yet given, but the oneness argument covers it by implication.
4
Note the footnote critique
The first footnote criticizes the phrase 'no multiplicity in essence' because a partner is separate, not an internal multiplicity. This highlights the precision needed in theological language.

Commentary
This passage is a statement and partial proof of God's transcendence from a partner (sharīk). It explains that 'partner' means one who shares with God absolutely—in essence, attributes, or actions. Consequently, there is no multiplicity in essence, no equal in attributes, and no independent creation by any other. The proof for the first two parts is said to have already been given in the discussion of oneness (tawḥīd). The footnotes note a criticism: the phrase 'no multiplicity in essence' is not exactly about a partner (which is separate), and that the proof for no partner in actions had not yet been given earlier.

Transcendence from Parent, Child, and Companions
Argument for God's transcendence from parental and filial relations, refuting Christian doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the doctrine of God's absolute transcendence refutes the Christian concept of Jesus as the son of God, based on the impossibility of parent-child relationships for the Creator.

Core Idea
God has no parent, no child, and no companion.

This lesson examines a passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid commentary that establishes God's transcendence from relational bonds like parenthood and friendship. The author uses a simple logical argument: having a parent or child implies separation from another being, which is impossible for God. The footnote reveals that this directly targets Christian claims about Jesus being divine son of God. Though the passage on 'companions' is incomplete, the main refutation is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

The passage is partially incomplete due to OCR, but the core argument is intact.
2
Understand the logical chain
Identify the two-part argument: (1) God cannot have a parent because that would imply separation, (2) God cannot have a child because that would imply separation. Both are impossible for the transcendent Creator.
The author uses the concept of 'connection' and 'separation' (الاتصال والانفصال).
3
Connect to the refutation of Christians
The footnote explicitly states that this argument refutes Christians who believe Jesus is divine and a son of God. Reflect on why this refutes the Trinity.
4
Consider the incomplete part on 'companions'
The passage begins to address 'companions' (أصدقاء) but is cut off. Think about how the same logic would apply: God cannot have equals or associates.
This connects to the broader Islamic refutation of shirk (associating partners with God).

Divine Transcendence

God's Transcendence from Having a Partner
Defines God's transcendence from having a partner in essence, attributes, and actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's transcendence from having a partner encompasses His essence, attributes, and actions, and that the proof for this is the same as the proof for His oneness.

Core Idea
God has no partner—not in essence, attributes, or actions.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes the scope of tawhid: it is not just about God being one in Himself, but also about His attributes and actions being uniquely His. The text clarifies what 'partner' means and ties the proof to the earlier argument for oneness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'partner'
Read the passage and note that 'partner' (sharik) is defined as anyone who shares in God's essence, attributes, or actions. This is a broad and absolute denial.
2
Identify the three aspects
The passage explicitly states that there is no multiplicity in essence, no equal in attributes, and no creation by other than Him in actions. These are the three areas where partnership is negated.
3
Connect to the proof from oneness
The text says the proof for all three is the same as what preceded regarding the necessity of oneness (wajib al-wahdaniyya). Reflect on how the proof for tawhid automatically denies any partner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنَزُّهه سبحَانَهُ عَنِ الشَّرِيك
لوجوب مخالفته تعالى للممكنات ذاتا وصفات (۱).
وحالَ كونه تعالى مُنزّها أيضًا عن (شَريكِ ) أي : مُشارك له (مُطْلَقًا ) أي : في ذاته ، أو في صفاته ، أو في أفعاله، فلا تكثر في ذاته (٢) ، ولا نظير له في صفاته ، ولا اختراع لغيره في أفعاله ، ودليل هذا (۳) ما مرَّ في وجوب الوحدانية له تعالى .
(۱) قوله: (ذاتا) وصفات أي وأفعالا ، وإنما ترك ذكر الأفعال لأن حكمها يُعلم من برهان الوحدانية . الشنواني .
(۲) قوله: (فلا تكثر في ذاته هذا ليس مناسبا لحل المتن لأن الكلام في الشريك ؛ وهو لا يكون إلا منفصلا. الشنواني.
(۳) قوله ودليل) (هذا أي بعض هذا، وهو أنه لا شريك له في الذات، ولا نظير له الصفات، إذ هو الذي تقدم دليله ، وأما عدم الشريك في الأفعال فلم يتقدم دليله. الشنواني.
His transcendence, glory be to Him, from the partner. Due to the necessity of His exaltedness being different from contingent beings in essence and attributes (1). And the state of Him, exalted, being also transcendent from (a partner), meaning someone sharing with Him, (absolutely), meaning in His essence, or in His attributes, or in His actions. Thus there is no multiplicity in His essence (2), no similar in His attributes, no creation by other than Him in His actions, and the proof of this (3) is what preceded concerning the necessity of oneness for Him, exalted. (1) His saying: (essence) and attributes means and actions, and he only omitted mentioning actions because their ruling is known from the proof of oneness. Al-Shanwani. (2) His saying: (so there is no multiplicity in His essence), this is not appropriate for the resolution of the text because the discussion is about the partner, and he is not but separate. Al-Shanwani. (3) His saying (and the proof) (this means some of this, which is that there is no partner for Him in essence, and no similar in attributes, for its proof has preceded, but the absence of a partner in actions has not had its proof precede. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage defines and proves God's transcendence from having any partner. It begins by stating the principle that God is transcendent from the partner (sharik), then explains that 'partner' means any participant in essence, attributes, or actions—thus no multiplicity in essence, no equal in attributes, and no other creator in actions. The proof for all three aspects is the same as the proof for God's oneness (tawhid), which has been previously established. The footnotes clarify: the first notes that 'attributes' in the phrase 'essence and attributes' includes actions; the second points out that the phrase 'no multiplicity in essence' is not the best expression here because the partner is separate, not internal; the third explains that the proofs for the first two aspects have preceded, but the proof for the absence of a partner in actions has not yet been given.
